<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com</a>)</p>
<p>COURSE OULTLINE</p>
<p>EME 2101 Engineering Drawing &amp; Design I</p>
<p>Purpose</p>
<p>The aim of this course is to enable the student to;</p>
<ol>
<li>understand basic aspects of engineering drawing practice,</li>
<li>gain skills of engineering drawing and sketching,</li>
<li>understand the aspects of design.</li>
</ol>
<p>Learning outcomes</p>
<p>At the end of this unit, the student should be able to:</p>
<ol>
<li>select and use appropriate drawing instruments for a particular drawing task and construct loci of points in mechanisms commonly encountered in mechanical engineering</li>
<li>make orthographic drawings given pictorial drawings, interpret orthographic drawings, and make isometric and oblique drawings/sketches for given orthographic drawings</li>
<li>make freehand sketches and construct thread forms, draw various types of fasteners and gear tooth profiles.</li>
</ol>
<p>Course description</p>
<p>Various aspects of graphic language. Aesthetics, artistic and technical drawing. Technical drawings: Technical drawing equipment for pencil work and ink work, drawing paper sizes, lettering and linework. Introduction to orthographic projections. Three-dimensional views: isometric, perspective and oblique. Construction of loci. Free hand sketching, sketching materials, isometric grid paper, scaling and proportions. Types of sketches: Isometric, oblique and perspective. One, two and multi-view sketches. Aspects of design. Different types of thread forms. Cams and gear teeth profiles. Various types of fasteners, shaft and couplings. Conventional representation of features including British Standard (BS) 308, International Standard organization (ISO) 4500 and Kenya Bureau of Standards (KEBS) codes of drawing practice.</p>
<p>Prerequisites: None</p>
<p>Prescribed text books</p>
<ol>
<li>Morling K. (1974) Geometric and Engineering Drawing, Butterworth-Heinemann, 2 Ed.</li>
<li>Eide A.R., Jenism R.D. &amp; Mashaw L.H. (1995) Engineering graphics fundamentals, McGraw-Hill, inc. 2nd Ed.</li>
</ol>
<p>References</p>
<ol>
<li>Thomas E.F., Jay D.H., Byron U. &amp; Carl L. S. (1997) Mechanical drawing CAD-communications, Mc Graw-Hill 11th Ed.</li>
<li>Giesecke F.E., Hill I.L., Norak J.E. &amp; Mitchel A. (1991) Technical Drawing, Prenctice-Hall, inc.</li>
<li>Journal of Mechanical Design</li>
</ol>
<p>Teaching methodology: 2 hour lecture and 3 hour practice per week</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chapter-1-introduction">Chapter 1 Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chapter-1-introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-1-graphics-language">1.1 Graphics Language<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-1-graphics-language" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Graphics are visual presentations on some surface such as a wall, paper, canvas, computer screen, etc, to inform (communicate) or to entertain. Examples of graphics are:</p>
<ul>
<li>pictures</li>
<li>photographs</li>
<li>visual art</li>
<li>diagrams</li>
<li>engineering drawings, etc</li>
</ul>
<p>The graphic language is the idea of communicating thoughts from one person to another by means of graphics. The role of the graphics language is:</p>
<ul>
<li>To communicate ideas</li>
<li>To communicate feelings</li>
<li>To convey information</li>
</ul>
<p>Written language is essential for discussing concepts, meanings or feelings, the things that do not have form, whereas things that have form are better explained or captured with the graphic language. Just as a person must be literate in order to to understand the written language, visual literacy is also important in order to understand information in graphic form.</p>
<p>Communication by use of graphics has developed along two distinct paths:</p>
<ul>
<li>Artistic drawing</li>
<li>Technical drawing</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="1-1-1-artistic-drawing">1.1.1 Artistic Drawing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-1-1-artistic-drawing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<p>Artistic drawings are used to express aesthetic, philosophic or other abstract ideas. They can also be used to portray decorative aspects, to express joy, sorrow, political or religious beliefs. It can be carried out on almost any surface and allows many interpretations. Common tools for artistic drawing are:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>graphite pencils</p>
</li>
<li></li>
</ul>
<p>EME 2101: 1.1 Graphics Language
2</p>
<ul>
<li>inked brushes</li>
<li>crayons</li>
<li>charcoal</li>
<li>markers</li>
<li>waxed colored pencils, etc.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-1-2-introduction-to-technical-drawing">1.1.2 Introduction to Technical Drawing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-1-2-introduction-to-technical-drawing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Technical drawing (drafting) is the practice of creating accurate descriptions and representations of existing or imagined physical objects for technical, architectural and engineering needs. They are used in development of ideas for designs and to convey technical information. Aspects of a technical drawing include:</p>
<ul>
<li>shape</li>
<li>size</li>
<li>material</li>
<li>assembly</li>
<li>finish</li>
</ul>
<p>Essential requirements for a technical drawing include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Clear and unambiguous</li>
<li>Language independent</li>
<li>accurate</li>
<li>Conform to standards</li>
<li>suitable for duplication</li>
</ul>
<p>There are three types of technical drawings:</p>
<ol>
<li>Multi-view drawings (orthographic); one or more views (projections) systematically arranged.</li>
<li>Pictorial; one view drawn in 3D at a specified angle as it would appear in real life.</li>
<li>Schematic (diagrammatic); simplified functional drawing.</li>
</ol>
<p>This document is available on
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com</a>)</p>
<p>EME 2101: 1.1 Graphics Language
3</p>
<p>Drawing conventions (drawing grammar) allow people to communicate more effectively and with clarity. These conventions are set by various standards organizations such as:</p>
<ul>
<li>ISO International Organization for Standardization</li>
<li>ISO 128: Technical Drawings - General principles of presentation.</li>
<li>ISO 129: Technical Drawings - Dimensioning</li>
<li>ISO 3098/1: Technical Drawings - Lettering</li>
<li>ISO 8048: Technical Drawings - Construction Drawings - Representation of views, sections, cuts.</li>
<li>KEBS Kenya Bureau of Standards</li>
<li>KS 06-325. Year 1986. Title: Engineering drawing practice. scope: Specifies the general principles of presentation and practice to be applied to engineering drawings. Subject: Engineering drawings</li>
<li>KS 06-1334 Year 1996. Title: Specification for engineer's squares.</li>
<li>ANSI American National Standards Institute</li>
<li>BS British Standards</li>
</ul>
<p>These standards ensure quality, safety and interchangeability of products and information. Standards are updated periodically rendering the old ones obsolete.</p>
<p>Technical drawing is referred to as the universal language for Engineering since it can be interpreted in any part of the world.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-1-3-drawing-equipment">1.1.3 Drawing Equipment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-1-3-drawing-equipment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>

<li>T-square</li>
<li>Set of instruments (draughtsman set)</li>

<li>Triangle 60°-60°-90°</li>
<li>Protractor</li>
<li>Pencils HB, H, 2H, 4H</li>
</ol>

<ol>

<li>Speed - for productivity</li>
<li>Legibility - (clear and legible for communication)</li>
<li>Neatness</li>
<li>Use only one side of the drawing paper.</li>
</ol>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="1-2-2-drawing-layout">1.2.2 Drawing layout<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-2-2-drawing-layout" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The Inside border encloses the working area, including the title block and other tables. The border shall be  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>20</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">20\mathrm{mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">20</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span>  wide on the binding edge and  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>10</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">10\mathrm{mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">10</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span>  wide on the other three sides, with resulting working area sizes as shown in Tables 1 and 2. The border on the binding edge allows for binding or hole punching for filing purposes. The drawing layouts can be in two formats:</p>
<ul>
<li>portrait, the longer edge is vertical</li>
</ul>

<p>EME 2101: 1.2 Paper sizes, lettering and line types</p>
<ul>
<li>landscape, the longer edge is horizontal</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="1-2-3-title-block">1.2.3 Title Block<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-2-3-title-block" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A title block is a system of labeling and cross-referencing drawings so that the may be stored and when required, identified and located easily and efficiently. Title blocks are used to record all the important information necessary for the working drawings. It contains general as well as specific information. The basic requirements for a title block located at the bottom right hand corner of a drawing are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Title of the drawing</li>
<li>Drafters name and number</li>
<li>Name of firm or organization</li>
<li>Scale</li>
<li>Date</li>
<li>Dimensions (units of measurements) used</li>
<li>Drawing number or projection symbol</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-c375aaa9.jpeg" alt="img-3.jpeg">
Figure 1.3 shows a sample title block
Figure 1.3: Sample title block</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="1-2-4-lettering">1.2.4 Lettering<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-2-4-lettering" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Lettering is used to give dimensions and other pertinent information of what is being drawn. ANSI recommends that Single Stroke Gothic style be the accepted lettering standard due to the following reasons:</p>
<ul>
<li>Most plain lettering style</li>
</ul>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id=""><span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"></span></h1></div>

<ul>
<li>Written rapidly
Highly legible</li>

<p>Single stroke lettering has all the strokes of uniform thickness, and each stroke is produced by one movement of the pencil. Figure 1.4 shows sample lettering using Gothic style. Guidelines for</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-04ee2a24.jpeg" alt="img-4.jpeg">
Figure 1.4: Lettering using Gothic style</p>
<p>lettering in technical drawing:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Use H or HB pencils when lettering</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Pencils should be sharpened to a conical point</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Good lettering requires the use of guidelines drawn using construction lines (4H pencil)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Characters should have the simplest form possible</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Letter spacing - use uniform area between letters and not uniform spacing</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Word and sentence spacing - should be equivalent to the letter height</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Letter height</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>6</mn><mrow><mtext>&nbsp;</mtext><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">6 \mathrm{~mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">6</span><span class="mord"><span class="mspace nobreak">&nbsp;</span><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span>  for upper case letters</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>4</mn><mrow><mtext>&nbsp;</mtext><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">4 \mathrm{~mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">4</span><span class="mord"><span class="mspace nobreak">&nbsp;</span><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span>  for lower case letters</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>For dimensions and notes, a character height of  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>3</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">3\mathrm{mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">3</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span>  should be used.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Spacing between lines of letters should be at least  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mfrac><mn>2</mn><mn>3</mn></mfrac></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\frac{2}{3}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1.1901em; vertical-align: -0.345em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mopen nulldelimiter"></span><span class="mfrac"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8451em;"><span style="top: -2.655em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">3</span></span></span></span><span style="top: -3.23em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="frac-line" style="border-bottom-width: 0.04em;"></span></span><span style="top: -3.394em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">2</span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.345em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose nulldelimiter"></span></span></span></span></span></span>  the letter height</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Lettering guides can be used (AMES lettering guide)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>fraction:  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mfrac><mn>4</mn><mn>5</mn></mfrac></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\frac{4}{5}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1.1901em; vertical-align: -0.345em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mopen nulldelimiter"></span><span class="mfrac"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8451em;"><span style="top: -2.655em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">5</span></span></span></span><span style="top: -3.23em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="frac-line" style="border-bottom-width: 0.04em;"></span></span><span style="top: -3.394em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">4</span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.345em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose nulldelimiter"></span></span></span></span></span></span>  not  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>4</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">/</mi><mn>5</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">4/5</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mord">4/5</span></span></span></span></span></p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="1-2-5-line-types">1.2.5 Line Types<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-2-5-line-types" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Thin line lines that are dark, and drawn with 2H pencil include: centerlines, extension lines, dimension lines, leader lines, section lines, hidden lines.</p>
<p>Thick lines that are dark and drawn with a HB pencil include; outlines, visible lines.</p>

<p>Very thick lines that are dark and drawn with a B pencil include: cutting plane lines, viewing plane lines.</p>
<p>Thin Light lines drawn with a 4H pencil include: Guidelines used for lettering, construction lines</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chapter-2-orthographic-projection">Chapter 2 Orthographic Projection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chapter-2-orthographic-projection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-1-introduction">2.1 Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-1-introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>A view of an object is known technically as a projection. Orthographic projection is a system of drawing views of an object using perpendicular projectors from the object to the projection plane. The object is positioned in such a way that the principal face is parallel to the projection plane. The projected view describes the exact shape of the object as seen in a specified direction. There are three planes for an orthographic projection:</p>
<ol>
<li>Frontal plane - plane on which the front view is projected.</li>
<li>Horizontal plane - plane on which the top or bottom view is projected.</li>
<li>profile plane - plane on which the end view is projected.</li>
</ol>
<p>Once the views are projected onto the respective planes, the planes are opened out so that they lie on the same plane. This produces a number of separate two-dimensional inter-related views which represent faces that are mutually at right angles to each other. Several views of an object can therefore be shown simultaneously on a single drawing paper.</p>
<p>Figure 2.1 shows how views of an object are obtained by using projectors. If the planes of projection are parallel to the principal faces of the object, they form a glass box. Note that the object has three principal dimensions: <em>width, height</em> and <em>depth</em>. These are fixed terms used for dimensions in these directions regardless of the shape of the object. Since it is required to show the views of a solid or 3D object on flat sheet of paper, it is necessary to unfold the planes so that they all lie in the same plane. Since the glass box has six sides, we can have six views of the object as shown in figure 2.2.</p>
<p>EME 2101: 2.1 Introduction</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-cca5b78b.jpeg" alt="img-5.jpeg">
Figure 2.1: Projection of views on a glass box.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-2-visualizing-the-views">2.2 Visualizing the Views<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-2-visualizing-the-views" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The mental process of reading a reading a drawing in order to obtain the views is known as visualizing the views. Figure 2.3 shows the process of visualization.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-3-types-of-orthographic-projections">2.3 Types of Orthographic Projections<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-3-types-of-orthographic-projections" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Orthographic projection uses two main principle planes of projection; the frontal plane and the vertical plane. These planes intersect to produce four quadrants or angles as shown in figure 2.4. The object to be drawn is imagine to be placed in one of these quadrants and orthographic views of it are projected onto these planes. In practice, only the first and third angles are used since views in the second and fourth quadrants may overlap.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com</a>)</p>
<p>EME 2101: 2.3 Types of Orthographic Projections</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-d81a4088.jpeg" alt="img-8.jpeg">
Figure 2.4: Principal planes of projection</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="2-3-1-first-angle-orthographic-projection">2.3.1 First Angle Orthographic Projection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-3-1-first-angle-orthographic-projection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Figure 2.5 shows an object positioned in the First quadrant. Views of the have been drawn on the plane using projectors. The view on the frontal plane is called the elevation, that on the horizontal plane the plan and that on the profile plane the end view, end elevation or side elevation. The observer always looks through the object and to the planes of projection. To obtain the views, the horizontal and profile planes are opened out (or rabatted) about the line of intersection with the profile plane.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="2-3-2-third-angle-orthographic-projection">2.3.2 Third Angle Orthographic Projection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-3-2-third-angle-orthographic-projection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This is done by positioning the object in the third quadrant and projecting the views onto the planes as shown in figure 2.6. Since the planes now come between the observer and the object, they are imagined to be transparent and the object is viewed through them. The views are the same in both systems and the only ultimate difference between the two systems is the arrangement of the views.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-d1ad7e9b.jpeg" alt="img-10.jpeg">
Figure 2.6: Third angle orthographic projection</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="2-3-3-projection-symbols">2.3.3 Projection Symbols<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-3-3-projection-symbols" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Since two systems of projection,  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>1</mn><mrow><mi>s</mi><mi>t</mi></mrow></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">1^{st}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7936em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">1</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.7936em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mathnormal mtight">s</span><span class="mord mathnormal mtight">t</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>  and  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>3</mn><mrow><mi>r</mi><mi>d</mi></mrow></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">3^{rd}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8491em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">3</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8491em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mathnormal mtight" style="margin-right: 0.02778em;">r</span><span class="mord mathnormal mtight">d</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>  angle are approved internationally, it is necessary to indicate on the drawing which system has been used. This is done by a symbol consisting of the an elevation and end view of a frustum of a cone. This symbols are shown in figures 2.5 and 2.6.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="2-3-4-comparison-between-first-and-third-angle-projections">2.3.4 Comparison between First and Third Angle Projections<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-3-4-comparison-between-first-and-third-angle-projections" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="2-3-5-two-view-mechanical-drawing">2.3.5 Two View Mechanical Drawing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-3-5-two-view-mechanical-drawing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>It may not be necessary to show all the six views to completely describe an object. Two views are sometimes enough to completely describe an object. This convention is particularly applied to symmetrical objects with less features. If a view does not show any additional features to the first two views, it should be excluded. If only two views are necessary and the top view and right side view are equally descriptive, the combination chosen is that which spaces best on the paper.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="view-selection-guidelines">View selection guidelines<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#view-selection-guidelines" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>The principle views should be chosen to show the most detail of the object and with the least number of hidden lines.</li>
<li>The most descriptive view should be selected as the front view</li>
<li>Build up all the views together by projecting measurements from one view to another.</li>
<li>Always allow enough space between the views on a drawing to accommodate dimensions and notes without crowding.  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>C</mi><mo>≥</mo><mn>15</mn><mrow><mtext>&nbsp;</mtext><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">C \geq 15 \mathrm{~mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8193em; vertical-align: -0.136em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.07153em;">C</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">≥</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">15</span><span class="mord"><span class="mspace nobreak">&nbsp;</span><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span> .</li>
</ul>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="2-3-6-three-view-mechanical-drawing">2.3.6 Three View Mechanical Drawing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-3-6-three-view-mechanical-drawing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>

<p>EME 2101: 2.4 Dimensioning</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="2-4-dimensioning">2.4 Dimensioning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-4-dimensioning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>An engineering drawing must be properly dimensioned in order to convey the designers intent to the end user. Dimensions provide the information needed to specify the size and location of every feature on the object. A properly dimensioned drawing helps ensure that the part produced in the manufacturing phase matches the part you asked for. There are a few simple guidelines to be followed when dimensioning a drawing and these guidelines cover the majority of cases you will encounter. Before we can begin to go over the rules for dimensioning, we must learn a little bit about the anatomy of a dimension. Figure 2.15 shows a dimensioned view of an object.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-e7e9b9a5.jpeg" alt="img-23.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-18b39fa8.jpeg" alt="img-24.jpeg">
Figure 2.15: The anatomy of a dimension 2.15</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="2-4-1-guidelines-for-dimensioning">2.4.1 Guidelines for Dimensioning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-4-1-guidelines-for-dimensioning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Dimensions should be placed outside the outline of the view wherever possible. This is achieved by drawing projection or extension lines from points or lines on the view and placing a dimension line between them. There should be a small gap between the outline and the start of the extension line and the extension line should continue a short distance beyond the dimension line. The dimension line has arrow heads about  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>3</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">3\mathrm{mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">3</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span>  long at each end and should touch the extension line or other limiting line.</li>
<li>The dimension line which is nearest to the outline should be about  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>10</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">10\mathrm{mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">10</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span>  from it if possible and succeeding dimensions should be well spaced for clarity.</li>
<li>The dimension should be applied in the view that provides the best description of the feature being dimensioned. For example, holes should be dimensioned in a view where they appear round. A slot should be dimensioned in a view where the contour of the slot is visible. Apply dimensions in a view where the feature appears true size.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com</a>)</p>
<p>EME 2101: 2.5 Drawing scales
24</p>
<ul>
<li>Place larger dimensions towards the outside so that extension lines don't cross dimension lines.</li>
<li>Do not over-dimension. Each feature should be dimensioned once and only once.</li>
<li>Use the diameter dimension to specify the size of holes and cylinders. Precede the dimension with the diameter symbol, <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>ϕ</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\phi</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8889em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">ϕ</span></span></span></span></span>.</li>
<li>Use the radius to dimension an arc. The radius dimension is preceded by the symbol, R. A leader line is commonly used for diameters and radii. The leader line should be a radial line directed through the center of the arc or circle.</li>
<li>Concentric circles should be dimensioned in a longitudinal view (figure 2.15).</li>
<li>Use the times symbol, <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo>×</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\times</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6667em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord">×</span></span></span></span></span>, to indicate repeated dimensions or features.</li>
<li>Dimensions should be arranged such that they can be read from the bottom of the drawing or from the right hand end of the drawing.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-5-drawing-scales">2.5 Drawing scales<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-5-drawing-scales" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Most drawings are made to full size, but if the size of an object makes it impossible to draw to full size, it can be drawn in proportion, that is to a uniform scale. The ratio of reduction or enlargement often depends upon the relative sizes of the object and of the sheet of paper upon which the drawing is to be made. Scales can be classified into 5:</p>
<ul>
<li>metric scale - the primary unit of measurement for engineering drawings is millimeter (mm)</li>
<li>engineers scale - the engineers scale is graduated in units of one inch divided into 10, 20, 30, 40, 50, and 60 parts.</li>
<li>decimal scale - on the full scale, one inch is divided into fiftieths of an inch, or 0.02.</li>
<li>mechanical engineers scale - mechanical engineers scales are divided into units representing inches to full size, half size, quarter size or eighth size.</li>
<li>architects scale - is primarily intended for drawing buildings, piping systems, and other large structures which must be drawn to a reduced scale to fit on a sheet of paper.</li>
</ul>
<p>The scale used must be stated on the drawing as a ratio for example 1:2 which means half full size and each division on a metric scale equals <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>2</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">2\mathrm{mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">2</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span> with the calibration numbering at 20-unit intervals. Drawings can also be drawn in an enlarged form for example 2:1 which means the drawing has been drawn twice the full size. Scale multipliers and divisors of 2, 5 and 10 are recommended for most commonly used scales eg.</p>
<ul>
<li>1:1 full size</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com</a>)</p>
<p>EME 2101: 2.6 Hidden detail Format</p>
<p>1:2 half full size
1:5 one-fifth full size
1:10 one-tenth full size
2:1 twice full size</p>
<ul>
<li>5:1 five times full size
10:1 10 times full size.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="2-6-hidden-detail-format">2.6 Hidden detail Format<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-6-hidden-detail-format" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Hidden lines are used to conceal edges that are not visible from a given viewing point. A hidden lines consists of medium lines with short evenly spaced dashes. Hidden lines should join visible lines except where the hidden line forms a continuation of the visible line. In this case, a gap should be left. Hidden detail lines should intersect to form L and T corners as shown:</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-9f40f5b8.jpeg" alt="img-25.jpeg">
Figure 2.16: Hidden line practices</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="2-7-true-lengths">2.7 True Lengths<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-7-true-lengths" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>It is important to be able to find the true length of a line in some aspects of Engineering Drawing, particularly in the development, or laying out in a plane, of sheet metals. In orthographic</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com</a>)</p>
<p>EME 2101: 2.7 True Lengths</p>
<p>projection, the projection of a line will only show its true length if the line is parallel to the plane on which it is projected. When a line is not parallel to one of the normal planes of projection, two methods may be used to find its true length:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="exercise">Exercise<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#exercise" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Referring to figure 2.17, A is on the frontal plane, B is on the profile plane and C is on the horizontal plane. Find the true lengths of the lines AB and BC and the true distance AC. Also find the true inclinations of AB, BC and AC to the horizontal plane. Use first angle projection.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-70a2c47f.jpeg" alt="img-26.jpeg">
Figure 2.17: True length of a line</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="1-revolution-method">1. Revolution method<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-revolution-method" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The line may be moved until it is parallel to a principle plane, or an auxiliary plane may be used which is parallel to the plan or elevation of the line. On the plan (figure 2.18), End A of the line is held stationary and end B is revolved until the line is parallel to the vertical plane. In one view, B moves along a circle while in the other, it moves along a straight line. There are two positions of the circle depending on the direction of rotation of B.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Triangulation Method To find the true length by triangulation, the plan length  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi><mo mathvariant="normal" lspace="0em" rspace="0em">′</mo></msup><msup><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi><mo mathvariant="normal" lspace="0em" rspace="0em">′</mo></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{A}^{\prime}\mathrm{C}^{\prime}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7519em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">A</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.7519em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">′</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">C</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.7519em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">′</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>  of the line is set off at right angles to the difference in vertical heights of the ends of the line in elevation. The hypotenuse  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi><mrow><mo mathvariant="normal">′</mo><mo mathvariant="normal">′</mo></mrow></msup><msup><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi><mo mathvariant="normal" lspace="0em" rspace="0em">′</mo></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{A}^{\prime \prime}\mathrm{C}^{\prime}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7519em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">A</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.7519em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">′′</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">C</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.7519em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">′</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>  of the right angled triangle formed is the true length of the line</li>
</ol>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chapter-3">Chapter 3<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chapter-3" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="three-dimensional-views">Three Dimensional Views<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#three-dimensional-views" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="3-1-introduction">3.1 Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-1-introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Three dimensional drawing (pictorial drawing) show several faces of an object simultaneously, approximately as they appear to the observer. Pictorial drawings show only the appearances of parts but does not completely describe complex or detailed forms. Pictorial drawings enable a person without technical training to visualize the design represented. It also enables the designer to visualize the successive stages of the design and to develop it in a satisfactory manner. There are three principal types of pictorial drawings:</p>
<ol>
<li>Isometric</li>
<li>Oblique</li>
<li>Perspective</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="3-2-isometric-projection">3.2 Isometric Projection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-2-isometric-projection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Isometric means equal measure. An isometric projection is produced by placing the object so that its principle edges or axis make equal angles with the plane of projection. In this position, the edges of a cube would be projected equally and would make equal angles with each other  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msup><mn>120</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(120^{\circ})</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord">12</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span> . Figure 3.2 shows the projected view. In this case, the edges of the cube are inclined to the plane</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-5b5b12a2.jpeg" alt="img-28.jpeg">
Figure 3.1: isometric projection</p>
<p>of projection and are therefore foreshortened. Therefore to obtain the full size view, an isometric</p>

<p>scale is used. When the view is prepared with an ordinary scale, it is an isometric drawing, when prepared with an isometric scale, it is an isometric projection. Isometric drawings are much easier to execute and are satisfactory for all practical applications.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="3-2-1-objects-composed-of-entirely-isometric-lines">3.2.1 Objects composed of entirely isometric lines<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-2-1-objects-composed-of-entirely-isometric-lines" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>These objects are easily drawn as all dimensions in the orthographic views may be drawn directly onto the isometric view. Hidden detail is omitted unless it is essential to show the shape of the object. Figure 3.4 shows the procedures in drawing an isometric view.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="3-2-2-objects-with-non-isometric-lines">3.2.2 Objects with non-isometric lines<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-2-2-objects-with-non-isometric-lines" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Lines on an object which are located by angles are non-isometric lines. Angles cannot be laid off directly on an isometric drawing as they do not appear in their true sizes. Lines positioned by angles are drawn by fixing their ends using isometric lines as shown in figure 3.5.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-e5dc7b4f.jpeg" alt="img-38.jpeg">
Make an isometric drawing of the object shown with corners A at the bottom</p>

<p>EME 2101: 3.2 Isometric Projection</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="3-2-3-circles-in-isometric-projection">3.2.3 Circles in isometric projection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-2-3-circles-in-isometric-projection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Any circle on an isometric drawing will appear as an ellipse which may be drawn in several ways:</p>
<ol>
<li>circle construction by circumscribing square method</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-8dc6bc1b.jpeg" alt="img-43.jpeg">
Figure 3.6: Isometric drawing</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-3d9ece7e.jpeg" alt="img-44.jpeg"></p>
<ol start="2">
<li>approximate circle construction; the ellipse occurs so frequently on isometric drawings that an approximate method using circular arcs is often used to draw it. This is done by drawing an isometric square with sides equal to the diameter of the circle. The long diagonal is drawn and either B or D is joined to the midpoints of opposite sides. Where these lines cross the long diagonal are two arc centers. The other centers are corners B and D on the short diagonal. When drawing a cylinder, the centers of the other end are obtained by drawing isometric lines thru' centers B, D, X and Y and marking off the length L of the cylinder to obtain centers B', D', X' and Y'. The same method may be used to draw holes if both ends are visible.</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-76ba5562.jpeg" alt="img-45.jpeg">
Figure 3.7: Isometric circle drawing by circular arc approximation</p>

<p>EME 2101: 3.3 Oblique Projection</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-15d977d2.jpeg" alt="img-47.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-9ce254bd.jpeg" alt="img-48.jpeg">
Figure 3.9: Isometric drawing</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="3-3-oblique-projection">3.3 Oblique Projection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-3-oblique-projection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Oblique projection is a method of producing a pictorial view of an object. In oblique, the projectors are parallel to each other but oblique to the projection plane as opposed to isometric projection where the projectors are parallel and normal to the projection plane. The principal face is kept parallel to the projection plane.</p>
<p>In oblique drawing, surfaces that are parallel to the principal face appear as true shapes while lines which are perpendicular to the principal surfaces are drawn at a receding angle,  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>30</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">30^{\circ}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6741em;"></span><span class="mord">3</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> ,  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>45</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">45^{\circ}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6741em;"></span><span class="mord">4</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">5</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>  or  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>60</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">60^{\circ}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6741em;"></span><span class="mord">6</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> . The receding angle to be used depends on the shape of the object and location of its significant</p>

<p>features. A larger angle is used to show features on the top plane of an object while a smaller angle is used to show features on the side plane of an object.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-74c58e59.jpeg" alt="img-50.jpeg">
Figure 3.11: Angle of receding line</p>
<p>When the receding lines are drawn with their true lengths, the object appears distorted. The degree of distortion may be decreased by decreasing the length of receding lines. Scales of  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mfrac><mn>1</mn><mn>2</mn></mfrac></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\frac{1}{2}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1.1901em; vertical-align: -0.345em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mopen nulldelimiter"></span><span class="mfrac"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8451em;"><span style="top: -2.655em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">2</span></span></span></span><span style="top: -3.23em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="frac-line" style="border-bottom-width: 0.04em;"></span></span><span style="top: -3.394em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">1</span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.345em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose nulldelimiter"></span></span></span></span></span></span>  and  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mfrac><mn>3</mn><mn>4</mn></mfrac></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\frac{3}{4}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1.1901em; vertical-align: -0.345em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mopen nulldelimiter"></span><span class="mfrac"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8451em;"><span style="top: -2.655em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">4</span></span></span></span><span style="top: -3.23em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="frac-line" style="border-bottom-width: 0.04em;"></span></span><span style="top: -3.394em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">3</span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.345em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose nulldelimiter"></span></span></span></span></span></span>  full size are commonly used. When the receding lines are true lengths (drawn to full scale), the oblique drawing is called a cavalier projection.</p>
<p>When the receding lines are drawn to  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mfrac><mn>1</mn><mn>2</mn></mfrac></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\frac{1}{2}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1.1901em; vertical-align: -0.345em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mopen nulldelimiter"></span><span class="mfrac"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8451em;"><span style="top: -2.655em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">2</span></span></span></span><span style="top: -3.23em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="frac-line" style="border-bottom-width: 0.04em;"></span></span><span style="top: -3.394em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">1</span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.345em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose nulldelimiter"></span></span></span></span></span></span> , the drawing is commonly known as a cabinet projection. The term is attributed to the early use of this type of oblique drawing in the furniture industries.</p>
<p>The face of an object containing circles and arcs should be placed parallel to the plane of projection if possible. Circular features which are not parallel to the front plane appear elliptical. The longest dimension of the object should be placed parallel to the plane of projection if possible.</p>

<p>EME 2101: 3.4 Perspective Projection</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="3-4-perspective-projection">3.4 Perspective Projection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-4-perspective-projection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Perspective projection (central projection) is a form of pictorial representation of objects that closely approximates the view obtained by the human eye more than the other types of pictorial drawings. Perspective is of major importance to the architect, industrial engineer and civil engineer.</p>
<p>Perspective drawing involves four main elements</p>
<ol>
<li>the observers eye</li>
<li>the object being viewed</li>
<li>the plane of projection</li>
<li>the projectors from the observers eye to all points on the object</li>
</ol>
<p>The plane of projection is placed between the observer and the object and collective piercing points in the plane of projection of all the projectors produces the perspective.</p>
<p>Perspective drawings are normally classified according to the number of vanishing points required which in turn depends upon the position of the object with respect to the projection plane or picture plane.</p>
<ul>
<li>If the object is placed with one face parallel to the projection plane, only one vanishing point is required and the result is one point perspective or parallel perspective</li>
<li>If the object is situated at an angle to the projection plane, but with the vertical edges parallel to the projection plane, two vanishing points are required and the result is a two point perspective or angular perspective. This is the most common type of perspective drawing.</li>
<li>If the object is situated so that no system of parallel edges is parallel to the projection plane, three vanishing points are necessary and the result is a three point perspective</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
