<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chapter-4">Chapter 4<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chapter-4" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="loci">Loci<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#loci" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="4-1-introduction">4.1 Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-1-introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The locus of a point is the path traced by that point when moving under given conditions. Many curves can be classified as loci; ellipse, parabola, hyperbola, hypocycloid, cycloid,trochoid,involute, etc. The study of loci is especially important in machine design where it is often necessary to determine the space requirements for a linkage or the envelope of a mechanism for the provision of effective guards.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="4-2-involute">4.2 Involute<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-2-involute" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>An involute is the path traced by a point on a string as it unwinds from a line, a polygon, or a circle. The involute of a circle is the most important of these curves as it finds applications in the construction of involute gear teeth. The involute may be generated by points on a straight line which is rolled on a fixed circle. This can be done as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>set off equal distances 0-1, 1-2, 2-3 ... along the circumference of the circle.</li>
<li>draw a tangent at each division point</li>
<li>set off along each tangent, the length of the corresponding circular arc.</li>
<li>draw the required curve through the points set off on the several tangents A, B, C, D, ...</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-e8f6bb9d.jpeg" alt="img-61.jpeg">
Figure 4.1: involute curve</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com</a>)</p>
<p>EME 2101: 4.3 Cycloids</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="4-3-cycloids">4.3 Cycloids<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-3-cycloids" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A cycloid is the locus of points generated by a fixed point on the circumference of a circle as the circle rolls along a straight line. This curve can be obtained as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>Given the generating circle and the straight line AB tangent to it, make the distances CA and CB equal to half the circumference of the circle.</li>
<li>Divide these distances and each semicircle into 6 equal parts and number them consecutively as shown.</li>
<li>Suppose the circle rolls to the left, point 1 on the circumference move to point 1' on the line, the center will be at D and the generating point 6 will be at the same distance from line AB as point 5 is when the circle is i the home position.</li>
<li>To find point  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>p</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">p</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.625em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">p</span></span></span></span></span>  draw a line through point 5 parallel to line AB and intersect it with an arc drawn from center D with radius equal to that of the circle.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-82e82e29.jpeg" alt="img-62.jpeg">
Figure 4.2: cycloidal curve</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="4-4-trochoids">4.4 Trochoids<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-4-trochoids" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A trochoid is the locus of a point  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">P</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{P}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord mathrm">P</span></span></span></span></span>  at distance  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">b</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{b}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6944em;"></span><span class="mord mathrm">b</span></span></span></span></span>  from the center of a circle (of radius  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">r</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{r}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4306em;"></span><span class="mord mathrm">r</span></span></span></span></span> ) as the circle rolls along a straight line, where  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>b</mi><mo mathvariant="normal">≠</mo><mi>r</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">b \neq r</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8889em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">b</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel"><span class="mrel"><span class="mord vbox"><span class="thinbox"><span class="rlap"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8889em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="inner"><span class="mord"><span class="mrel"></span></span></span><span class="fix"></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mrel">=</span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4306em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.02778em;">r</span></span></span></span></span></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="ass-2-a">Ass 2(a)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ass-2-a" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Plot the locus of a point  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>p</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">p</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.625em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">p</span></span></span></span></span>  shown in figure 4.3 as the circle rolls along line AB for one complete revolution of the circle. Figure 4.4 shows the locus in ass 2(a).</p>
<p>Trochoidal curves find application in the construction of the fillet gear tooth profiles.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="4-5-parabola">4.5 Parabola<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-5-parabola" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A parabola is the curve traced by a set of points in a plane, that are equidistant from a point (focus) and a line (directrix). The parabola can be developed in various ways:</p>
<p>i. Given the focus and directrix AB, draw a line perpendicular to AB to cut AB at C. Divide this line into a number of equal parts. Draw lines parallel to AB through the</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="practical-applications">Practical Applications:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#practical-applications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The parabola is used for reflecting surfaces for light and sound</li>
<li>Curves of cables for suspension bridges</li>
<li>to show bending moment at any point on a uniformly loaded beam</li>
<li>To obtain the displacement diagram for uniformly accelerating motion.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-6-ellipse">4.6 Ellipse<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-6-ellipse" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>An ellipse is the curve generated by a point moving so that the sum of its distances from two points (the foci) is constant and equal to the major axis. Ellipse construction:</p>
<ol>
<li>Given the major and minor axis, draw concentric circles with the major and minor diameters. Divide the circles into a number of equal sectors. Draw horizontal lines from the division points on the minor circle to intersect a vertical line from a corresponding division point on the major circle. The point of intersection is a point on the ellipse. To find the foci F1 and F2, strike arcs with radius equal to half the major axis and with centers at the ends of the minor axis.</li>
</ol>
<p>This document is available on

Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com</a>)</p>

<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-cea66b93.jpeg" alt="img-67.jpeg">
Figure 4.7: ellipse construction given the major and minor diameters</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Given major and minor axis, or the conjugate diameters AB and CD, draw a rectangle or parallelogram with sides parallel to the axes respectively. Divide AO and AJ into the same number of equal parts and draw lines through these points. The intersection of like-numbered lines will be points on the ellipse.</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-ba3f67d0.jpeg" alt="img-68.jpeg"></p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chapter-5">Chapter 5<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chapter-5" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="cam-profiles">Cam profiles<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cam-profiles" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="5-1-introduction">5.1 Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-1-introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Cams are used to convert the input motion (usually rotary motion) to reciprocating motion by causing the follower to rise and fall in a predetermined manner. As the cam turns, in a circular motion, the cam follower traces out the surface of the cam, transmitting its motion into the required mechanism. Figure 5.1 shows a simple cam and follower used in a combustion engine.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-ae1611d6.jpeg" alt="img-78.jpeg">
Figure 5.1: Cam and follower</p>
<p>Typical example of their usage include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Textile machinery</li>
<li>Internal combustion engine</li>
<li>Control systems and devices</li>
<li>Food processing machines</li>
<li>Toys</li>
</ul>
<p>In all cam systems it is important that the follower is always in contact and following the motion of the cam. This is achieved in a number of ways including the following.</p>
<p>Gravity</p>
<ul>
<li>Using a mechanical constraint system i.e groove</li>
<li>Using a spring force</li>
<li>Using a pneumatic or hydraulic force</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com</a>)</p>
<p>EME 2101: 5.2 Displacement diagrams</p>
<p>The problem of the designer is to construct the cam profile necessary to obtain the desired motion of the follower.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="5-2-displacement-diagrams">5.2 Displacement diagrams<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-2-displacement-diagrams" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Since the motion of the follower is of primary importance, its rate of speed and its various positions should be carefully planned in a displacement diagram before the cam profile is constructed.</p>
<p>A displacement diagram is a curve showing the displacement of the follower as ordinates erected on a baseline that represents one revolution of the cam. The follower displacement should be drawn to scale but any convenient length can be used to represent  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>360</mn><mi>o</mi></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">360^o</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6644em;"></span><span class="mord">36</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6644em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mathnormal mtight">o</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>  of the cam rotation.</p>
<p>Types of follower motion:</p>
<ul>
<li>Uniform velocity; equal displacements of the follower during equal periods of time (equal change of cam angular displacement)</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-36b3e8bc.jpeg" alt="img-79.jpeg">
Figure 5.2: Uniform velocity displacement diagram</p>
<p>The main disadvantage of this type of motion is that it produces abrupt changes of movement in the follower at the beginning and end of both rise and fall.</p>
<ul>
<li>Simple harmonic motion; the displacement diagram is a sine curve. This motion provides the smoothest change of motion in the follower.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-99df5da5.jpeg" alt="img-80.jpeg">
Figure 5.3: Uniform velocity displacement diagram</p>
<ul>
<li>Uniform acceleration and retardation; this displacement diagram is parabolic. It gives a uniform rate of acceleration from the start to the midpoint and a similar uniform rate of retardation from the midpoint to the end of the movement.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id=""><span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"></span></h1></div>

<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com</a>)</p>
<p>EME 2101: 5.2 Displacement diagrams</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-037cb3eb.jpeg" alt="img-81.jpeg">
Figure 5.4: Uniform velocity displacement diagram</p>
<p>Cams are made in a variety of forms, including:</p>
<ul>
<li>A rotating disk or plate with the radial required profile;</li>
<li>A reciprocating wedge of the required shape.</li>
<li>A cylindrical barrel cam (drum) with a follower groove cut in the diameter</li>
<li>A cylinder with the required profile cut in the end (end cam);</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-243006ca.jpeg" alt="img-82.jpeg">
Figure 5.5: Types of cams</p>
<p>Cam followers can either be reciprocating or pivoting. There are various ways of transferring the motion from the cam to the follower which include:</p>
<ul>
<li>knife edge</li>
<li>flat face</li>
<li>roller</li>
<li>curved shoe or spherical.</li>
</ul>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="5-3-drawing-a-cam-profile-to-a-given-data">5.3 Drawing a cam profile to a given data<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-3-drawing-a-cam-profile-to-a-given-data" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The first stage in creation of a cam system is to create the displacement diagram.</p>
<ul>
<li>Draw the two centerlines of the cam to locate its center of rotation.</li>
<li>Draw the maximum and minimum diameter circles about the cam center. Divide these radially into sectors to correspond with the displacement divisions.</li>
<li>Strike arcs to cut the appropriate radial division</li>
<li>Draw a smooth curve through the intersection points</li>
<li>For roller ended followers, the baseline of the displacement diagram is set in line with the center line of the roller and the cam curve is drawn tangential to the series of plotted roller circles.</li>
</ul>

<li><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>240</mn><mi>o</mi></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">240^o</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6644em;"></span><span class="mord">24</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6644em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mathnormal mtight">o</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> Fall <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>60</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">60\mathrm{mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">60</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span> with uniform velocity
Least radius of cam = 25 mm
Direction of rotation of the cam is clockwise.
Cam shaft diameter = 18 mm</li>
</ul>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chapter-6">Chapter 6<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chapter-6" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="gear-teeth-profiles">Gear Teeth Profiles<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#gear-teeth-profiles" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="6-1-introduction">6.1 Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-1-introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Gears are machine components used to transmit motion or power from one machine part to another. They are the most common power transmitting components due to the following reasons:</p>
<ul>
<li>They provide a positive drive</li>
<li>They provide a constant transmission ratio</li>
<li>Compactness</li>
<li>Ease of production</li>
<li>Allow interchangeability</li>
</ul>
<p>Gears may be classified according to the position of the shafts they connect:</p>
<ol>
<li>Parallel axis shafts - spur gears, helical gears, herringbone gears</li>
<li>intersecting shafts - bevel gears, hypoid gears, worm and wheel.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="6-1-1-spur-gears">6.1.1 Spur Gears<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-1-1-spur-gears" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Spur gears have their teeth cut parallel to the axis of the gear. If two cylinders are positioned as shown in figure 6.1 and some pressure applied between them so as to maintain contact, then any rotating motion in one cylinder will be transmitted to the other by friction. However, a slight loading applied to the driven cylinder will cause it to slip. To overcome this slipping, matching teeth are cut in both cylinders transforming them into a pair of meshing gear wheels.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-3b4554f4.jpeg" alt="img-93.jpeg">
Figure 6.1: Gear teeth nomenclature</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id=""><span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"></span></h1></div>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com</a>)</p>
<p>EME 2101: 6.1 Introduction
61</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="involute-gear-terminology">Involute Gear Terminology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#involute-gear-terminology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The most common tooth form for the gear tooth flank is the involute, and when it is made in this form, the gears are known as involute gears.</p>
<ul>
<li>Pitch Circle is an imaginary circle corresponding to the circumference of the friction cylinders from which the gear is derived. Its diameter is designated as D. Given the module, m and the number of teeth on a gear wheel, N, the pitch diameter is obtained by the relation:</li>
</ul>
<div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mtable width="100%"><mtr><mtd width="50%"></mtd><mtd><mrow><mi>D</mi><mo>=</mo><mi>m</mi><mo>×</mo><mi>N</mi></mrow></mtd><mtd width="50%"></mtd><mtd><mtext>(6.1)</mtext></mtd></mtr></mtable><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">D = m \times N \tag{6.1}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.02778em;">D</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6667em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">m</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">×</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.10903em;">N</span></span><span class="tag"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mord text"><span class="mord">(</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">6.1</span></span><span class="mord">)</span></span></span></span></span></span></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Base circle the imaginary circle from which the involute profile is developed.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Circular pitch, p is the distance measured along the pitch circle from a point on one tooth to a corresponding point on the adjacent tooth. It includes one tooth and one space.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mtable width="100%"><mtr><mtd width="50%"></mtd><mtd><mrow><mi>p</mi><mo>=</mo><mi>π</mi><mfrac><mi>D</mi><mi>N</mi></mfrac></mrow></mtd><mtd width="50%"></mtd><mtd><mtext>(6.2)</mtext></mtd></mtr></mtable><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">p = \pi \frac{D}{N} \tag{6.2}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.625em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">p</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 2.0463em; vertical-align: -0.686em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.03588em;">π</span><span class="mord"><span class="mopen nulldelimiter"></span><span class="mfrac"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 1.3603em;"><span style="top: -2.314em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.10903em;">N</span></span></span><span style="top: -3.23em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="frac-line" style="border-bottom-width: 0.04em;"></span></span><span style="top: -3.677em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.02778em;">D</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.686em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose nulldelimiter"></span></span></span><span class="tag"><span class="strut" style="height: 2.0463em; vertical-align: -0.686em;"></span><span class="mord text"><span class="mord">(</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">6.2</span></span><span class="mord">)</span></span></span></span></span></span></div>
<p>where N is the number of teeth on the gear</p>
<ul>
<li>Module, m is the pitch diameter in mm divided by the number of teeth on the gear wheel.</li>
</ul>
<div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mtable width="100%"><mtr><mtd width="50%"></mtd><mtd><mrow><mi>m</mi><mo>=</mo><mfrac><mi>D</mi><mi>N</mi></mfrac></mrow></mtd><mtd width="50%"></mtd><mtd><mtext>(6.3)</mtext></mtd></mtr></mtable><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">m = \frac{D}{N} \tag{6.3}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4306em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">m</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 2.0463em; vertical-align: -0.686em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mopen nulldelimiter"></span><span class="mfrac"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 1.3603em;"><span style="top: -2.314em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.10903em;">N</span></span></span><span style="top: -3.23em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="frac-line" style="border-bottom-width: 0.04em;"></span></span><span style="top: -3.677em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.02778em;">D</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.686em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose nulldelimiter"></span></span></span><span class="tag"><span class="strut" style="height: 2.0463em; vertical-align: -0.686em;"></span><span class="mord text"><span class="mord">(</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">6.3</span></span><span class="mord">)</span></span></span></span></span></span></div>
<ul>
<li>Diametral pitch, P is the ratio of the number of teeth divided by the pitch diameter in inches</li>
</ul>
<div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mtable width="100%"><mtr><mtd width="50%"></mtd><mtd><mrow><mi>P</mi><mo>=</mo><mfrac><mi>N</mi><mi>D</mi></mfrac></mrow></mtd><mtd width="50%"></mtd><mtd><mtext>(6.4)</mtext></mtd></mtr></mtable><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">P = \frac{N}{D} \tag{6.4}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.13889em;">P</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 2.0463em; vertical-align: -0.686em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mopen nulldelimiter"></span><span class="mfrac"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 1.3603em;"><span style="top: -2.314em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.02778em;">D</span></span></span><span style="top: -3.23em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="frac-line" style="border-bottom-width: 0.04em;"></span></span><span style="top: -3.677em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.10903em;">N</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.686em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose nulldelimiter"></span></span></span><span class="tag"><span class="strut" style="height: 2.0463em; vertical-align: -0.686em;"></span><span class="mord text"><span class="mord">(</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">6.4</span></span><span class="mord">)</span></span></span></span></span></span></div>
<ul>
<li>Chordal thickness, t is the thickness of a tooth measured along a chord of the pitch circle</li>
</ul>
<div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mtable width="100%"><mtr><mtd width="50%"></mtd><mtd><mrow><mi>t</mi><mo>=</mo><mi>D</mi><mi>sin</mi><mo>⁡</mo><mrow><mo fence="true">(</mo><mfrac><msup><mn>90</mn><mi>o</mi></msup><mi>N</mi></mfrac><mo fence="true">)</mo></mrow></mrow></mtd><mtd width="50%"></mtd><mtd><mtext>(6.5)</mtext></mtd></mtr></mtable><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">t = D \sin \left(\frac{90^o}{N}\right) \tag{6.5}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6151em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">t</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 2.4em; vertical-align: -0.95em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.02778em;">D</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.1667em;"></span><span class="mop">sin</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.1667em;"></span><span class="minner"><span class="mopen delimcenter" style="top: 0em;"><span class="delimsizing size3">(</span></span><span class="mord"><span class="mopen nulldelimiter"></span><span class="mfrac"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 1.3414em;"><span style="top: -2.314em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.10903em;">N</span></span></span><span style="top: -3.23em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="frac-line" style="border-bottom-width: 0.04em;"></span></span><span style="top: -3.677em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">9</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6644em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mathnormal mtight">o</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.686em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose nulldelimiter"></span></span><span class="mclose delimcenter" style="top: 0em;"><span class="delimsizing size3">)</span></span></span></span><span class="tag"><span class="strut" style="height: 2.4em; vertical-align: -0.95em;"></span><span class="mord text"><span class="mord">(</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">6.5</span></span><span class="mord">)</span></span></span></span></span></span></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Addendum, a is the radial distance from the pitch circle to the tip of the tooth.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Dedendum, b is the radial distance from the pitch circle to the root of the gear tooth.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mtable width="100%"><mtr><mtd width="50%"></mtd><mtd><mrow><mi>b</mi><mo>=</mo><mi>a</mi><mo>+</mo><mi>c</mi></mrow></mtd><mtd width="50%"></mtd><mtd><mtext>(6.6)</mtext></mtd></mtr></mtable><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">b = a + c \tag{6.6}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6944em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">b</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6667em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">a</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">+</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4306em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">c</span></span><span class="tag"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mord text"><span class="mord">(</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">6.6</span></span><span class="mord">)</span></span></span></span></span></span></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Pressure angle <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>ϕ</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\phi</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8889em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">ϕ</span></span></span></span></span> is the angle that determines the direction of pressure between contacting gear teeth. It also determines the size of the base circle.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Clearance, c is the radial distance between the tip of a tooth and the root of a mating tooth space.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Center Distance, C The distance between the centers of two mating gears.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mtable width="100%"><mtr><mtd width="50%"></mtd><mtd><mrow><mi>C</mi><mo>=</mo><mfrac><mrow><msub><mi>D</mi><mi>g</mi></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mi>D</mi><mi>p</mi></msub></mrow><mn>2</mn></mfrac></mrow></mtd><mtd width="50%"></mtd><mtd><mtext>(6.7)</mtext></mtd></mtr></mtable><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">C = \frac{D_g + D_p}{2} \tag{6.7}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.07153em;">C</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 2.0463em; vertical-align: -0.686em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mopen nulldelimiter"></span><span class="mfrac"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 1.3603em;"><span style="top: -2.314em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">2</span></span></span><span style="top: -3.23em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="frac-line" style="border-bottom-width: 0.04em;"></span></span><span style="top: -3.677em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.02778em;">D</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.1514em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-left: -0.0278em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mathnormal mtight" style="margin-right: 0.03588em;">g</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.2861em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">+</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.02778em;">D</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.1514em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-left: -0.0278em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mathnormal mtight">p</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.2861em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.686em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose nulldelimiter"></span></span></span><span class="tag"><span class="strut" style="height: 2.0463em; vertical-align: -0.686em;"></span><span class="mord text"><span class="mord">(</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">6.7</span></span><span class="mord">)</span></span></span></span></span></span></div>
<p>The subscripts g and p refer to the gear and pinion respectively.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com</a>)</p>
<p>EME 2101: 6.1 Introduction</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-2f481ab2.jpeg" alt="img-94.jpeg">
Figure 6.2: Gear teeth nomenclature</p>
<p>Metric gear specification usually give the number of teeth, the module and the pressure angle. Imperial gear specification usually give the number of teeth, the diametral pitch and the pressure angle.</p>
<p>The pitch circle is always drawn in thin long chain line because it is a center line. It is common practice to show two teeth of one wheel meshing with three teeth of the other wheel, the remainder of both wheels being left in blank outline only. Other relevant data should be given in tabular form.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="6-1-2-to-draw-an-involute-gear-tooth">6.1.2 To Draw an Involute Gear Tooth<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-1-2-to-draw-an-involute-gear-tooth" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Draw the vertical center line of the gears (This passes through the centers of both gears)</li>
<li>Draw the horizontal line through the pitch point (a distance  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mfrac><mi>D</mi><mn>2</mn></mfrac></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\frac{D}{2}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1.2173em; vertical-align: -0.345em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mopen nulldelimiter"></span><span class="mfrac"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8723em;"><span style="top: -2.655em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">2</span></span></span></span><span style="top: -3.23em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="frac-line" style="border-bottom-width: 0.04em;"></span></span><span style="top: -3.394em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mathnormal mtight" style="margin-right: 0.02778em;">D</span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.345em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose nulldelimiter"></span></span></span></span></span></span>  from the center of the gear)</li>
<li>Draw the line of action (pressure line) through the pitch point at an angle  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>ϕ</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\phi</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8889em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">ϕ</span></span></span></span></span>  to the horizontal</li>
<li>Draw the base circle tangential to the pressure line. Draw the pitch circle. Calculate the addendum, dedendum and draw the addendum circle and the root circle</li>
<li>Set out and draw the involute on the base circle.</li>
<li>Copy this involute profile on to a tracing paper including a section of the base circle to aid in correct aligning of the tracing. Set the tracing with the gear profile passing through the pitch point and running from the base circle to the addendum circle.</li>
</ol>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id=""><span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"></span></h1></div>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com</a>)</p>
<p>EME 2101: 6.1 Introduction</p>
<ol start="7">
<li>From the pitch point step off along the pitch circle, the chordal thickness of the tooth. Turn the tracing over and draw the other half of the tooth profile. Join the profile from the base circle to the root circle using radial lines and include a fillet of radius  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>0.1</mn><mi>p</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">0.1p</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8389em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord">0.1</span><span class="mord mathnormal">p</span></span></span></span></span>  to round off the corner between the tooth flank and the dedendum circle. Line in the Tip of the tooth along the addendum circle and the root of the tooth space along the dedendum circle to complete the tooth.</li>
<li>Step off the chordal thickness and draw three more teeth.</li>
<li>Repeat the procedure to the mating gear and draw at least two more teeth. Figure 6.3 shows the completed gear teeth.</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-fd4e5ad1.jpeg" alt="img-95.jpeg">
Figure 6.3: Gear teeth drawing</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="6-1-3-fillet-construction">6.1.3 Fillet Construction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-1-3-fillet-construction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Calculate the fillet radius (0.1p).</li>
<li>Draw radial lines connecting the involute profile at the base. With center at the point of intersection of the radial line and the root circle and with the fillet radius (r), strike arcs A and B. With centers at A and B, and radius r, strike arcs to obtain the center of the fillet arc.</li>
</ol>

64</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>Draw the fillet arc using a visible line, Figure 6.4.</li>
</ol>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="6-1-4-to-draw-the-teeth-of-a-standard-gear-by-approximate-arc-method">6.1.4 To draw the teeth of a standard gear by approximate arc method<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-1-4-to-draw-the-teeth-of-a-standard-gear-by-approximate-arc-method" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Lay off the pitch circle, the root circle and the addendum circle</li>
<li>Starting with the pitch point, divide the pitch circle into distances equal to the circular thickness of the tooth</li>
<li>Draw the pressure line through the pitch point</li>
<li>Draw the base circle, tangential to the pressure line.</li>
<li>With the compass set to a radius equal to the distance from the pitch point to the end of the pressure line on the base circle (radius of curvature), strike arcs through the division points on the pitch circle, with the center on the base circle.</li>
<li>Darken the arcs for the tops of the teeth and bottom of the spaces and add tooth fillets to complete the gear teeth as shown in figure 6.5.</li>
</ol>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chapter-7">Chapter 7<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chapter-7" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="screw-threads">Screw Threads<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#screw-threads" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="7-1-introduction">7.1 Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#7-1-introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A screw thread is a helical groove which is cut, rolled, or sometimes cast on a cylinder or cylindrical hole. The threads are parallel to each other with those on the cylinder (screw) being external, and those in the hole (nut) being internal. Tapered threads are formed on a cone or in a conical hole. The form of the groove varies with different threads depending on the use. Threads may be right- or left-handed and single or multi-start. Screw threads have three main applications:</p>
<ul>
<li>Assembly or fastening parts together.</li>
<li>To adjust the position of parts.</li>
<li>Power transmission purposes.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="7-1-1-screw-thread-terms">7.1.1 Screw Thread Terms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#7-1-1-screw-thread-terms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-7a51f27f.jpeg" alt="img-99.jpeg">
Figure 7.1: Screw thread terminology</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-95764870.jpeg" alt="img-100.jpeg"></p>
<p>Figure 7.1 shows the primary terms useful in defining screw threads. These terms are:</p>
<ul>
<li>External thread (screw); A thread on the external surface of a cylinder.</li>
<li>Internal thread (nut); A thread on the internal surface of a cylinder.</li>
<li>Major Diameter; The largest diameter of a screw thread.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com</a>)</p>
<p>EME 2101: 7.1 Introduction
68</p>
<ul>
<li>Minor Diameter; The smallest diameter of a screw thread.</li>
<li>Pitch diameter; The diameter of an imaginary cylinder, the surface of which cuts the thread forms where the width of the thread and groove are equal.</li>
<li>Crest; The edge or surface that joins the sides of adjacent thread forms and is farthest from the cylinder axis</li>
<li>Root; The edge or surface that joins the sides of adjacent thread forms and coincides with the cylinder from which the thread projects.</li>
<li>Flanks; The straight sides which connect the crest and the root.</li>
<li>Pitch; The distance between corresponding points on adjacent thread forms measured parallel to the axis</li>
<li>Lead; The distance a threaded part moves axially with respect to a fixed mating part, in one complete revolution.</li>
<li>Form; The profile (cross-section) of the thread.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="7-1-2-thread-forms">7.1.2 Thread Forms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#7-1-2-thread-forms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>"Thread Form" describes the shape of the thread if one cut a thread form in half along its axis and then looked at the thread configurations. Different thread forms have different uses:</p>
<p>i. Metric Thread The ISO metric screw threads are the worlds most commonly used type of general purpose screw threads. The design principles of this thread form are defined in the standards ISO 68-1.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-a0ade54a.jpeg" alt="img-101.jpeg">
Figure 7.2: External ISO metric thread terms</p>
<p>Designation
This document is available on
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com</a>)</p>
<p>EME 2101: 7.1 Introduction</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-4bcab56e.jpeg" alt="img-102.jpeg">
Figure 7.3: Internal ISO metric thread</p>
<p>A metric ISO screw thread is designated by the letter M followed by the value of the nominal diameter D and the pitch, p, both expressed in mm and separated by a times sign e.g.  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>M</mi><mn>8</mn><mo>×</mo><mn>1.5</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">M8 \times 1.5</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7667em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.10903em;">M</span><span class="mord">8</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">×</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">1.5</span></span></span></span></span> , signifies that the thread has a nominal diameter of  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>8</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">8\mathrm{mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">8</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span>  and a pitch of  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>1.5</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">1.5\mathrm{mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">1.5</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span> . Applications include assembly and adjustment purposes. Other types of threads for general purpose applications include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Unified thread standard (UTS)- commonly used in the USA and Canada.</li>
<li>British Standard Whitworth - British standard fine threads and British standard cycle</li>
</ul>
<p>ii. Square thread This type of thread has its flanks normal to the axis thus making it the most ideal for power transmission purposes. However, the right angle flanks cause machining difficulties when the thread is cut on the lathe.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-36e05583.jpeg" alt="img-103.jpeg">
Figure 7.4: Square thread</p>
<p>iii. ACME thread This type is an adaptation of the square thread, with the flanks having a thread angle of  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>29</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">29^{\circ}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6741em;"></span><span class="mord">2</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">9</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> . It is also used for power transmission applications where the nut has to be disengaged from the screw.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com</a>)</p>
<p>EME 2101: 7.1 Introduction</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-458eb533.jpeg" alt="img-104.jpeg">
Figure 7.5: ACME thread</p>
<p>iv. Buttress thread The front face against which the load is applied is inclined at  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>7</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">7^{\circ}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6741em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">7</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>  to the vertical. The thread is used to transmit power in one direction only, indicated by the arrow on the diagram.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-97af5898.jpeg" alt="img-105.jpeg">
Figure 7.6: Buttress thread</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="7-1-3-conventional-representation-of-screw-threads">7.1.3 Conventional Representation of Screw Threads<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#7-1-3-conventional-representation-of-screw-threads" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Since projection of screw threads is tedious and takes considerable time, threads are shown conventionally on engineering drawings.</p>
<p>On the longitudinal view of an external thread, the major diameter is shown by a pair of thick lines and the minor diameter by a pair of thin lines. The end of a thread is shown by a thick line. The end of the full thread is drawn as a thick line. The thread run out (incompletely formed threads) are represented by thin lines at  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>30</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">30^{\circ}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6741em;"></span><span class="mord">3</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>  to the major diameter. On the circular view, the major diameter appears as a complete thick circle and the minor diameter as a thin circle with a gap as shown in figure 7.7. For internal threads in section the minor diameter drilling is drawn</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id=""><span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"></span></h1></div>
<p>studocu</p>

<p>EME 2101: 7.2 Threaded Fasteners</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-9f32f045.jpeg" alt="img-106.jpeg">
Figure 7.7: Conventional representation of crew threads</p>
<p>in thick lines. The major diameter and thread run outs are shown by thin lines. The hatching crosses the major diameter and terminates on the minor diameter. If an internal thread is shown in hidden detail, all lines are thin short dashes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="7-2-threaded-fasteners">7.2 Threaded Fasteners<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#7-2-threaded-fasteners" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Most engineering products are composed of separate parts held together by some means of fastening. Threaded have advantage over permanent methods (riveting and welding) in that they are easily disassembled. Threaded fasteners have descriptive names with five types being the most common;</p>
<ol>
<li>Bolt; A bolt, has an integral head on one end and a thread on the other end and is passed through clearance holes in two parts and draws them together by means of a nut screwed on the threaded end. It comes in a variety of head forms, the most common being the hexagonal head and the square head. On the sectional view, the nut and bolt are not sectioned even though the section plane passes through them. If the details being joined are of a soft material, a plain washer may be placed under the nut and sometimes under the bolt head as well to prevent damage when the nut is tightened.</li>
<li>Stud; A stud is a rod threaded on each end. The fastener passes through a clearance hole in one part and screws into a tapped hole in the other. A nut then draws the parts together. The stud is used when through bolts are not suitable for parts that must be removed frequently such as a cylinder head. They are also used in situations where there is insufficient space on one side of the assembly for bolt heads or nuts</li>
</ol>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com</a>)</p>
<p>EME 2101: 7.2 Threaded Fasteners</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-0f983fd4.jpeg" alt="img-107.jpeg">
Figure 7.8: Hexagonal bolt and nut assembly</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251223-a8fb5970.jpeg" alt="img-108.jpeg">
Figure 7.9: Front and side view of a hexagonal bolt and nut assembly</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>Cap screw A cap screw passes through a clearance hole in one piece and screws into a tapped hole in the other. The head, which is an integral part of the screw draws the parts together as the screw enters the tapped hole. A hexagonal head cap screw is similar to a bolt except that it generally has a greater length of thread.</li>
<li>Machine Screw Is a small fastener used with a nut to function in the same way as a bolt or without a nut to function as cap screw. machine screws are used in a limited number of diameter sizes. These come in ten standardized head shapes which except for the hexagonal head, are available in slotted form or with cross recesses. The size of the recess varies with the size of the screw. The hexagonal machine screw is not made with a cross recess but may be optionally slotted.</li>
</ol>

<li>Set screw A set screw screws into a tapped hole in an outer part, often a hub and bears with its point against an inner part, usually a shaft. Set screws hold two parts in relative</li>
