<p>Chapter 8 Design Phases and Free-Hand Sketching</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="8-1-aspects-of-design">8.1 Aspects of Design<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-1-aspects-of-design" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>To design is to formulate a plan for the satisfaction a human need. The design process provides a structure in which the various phases of design occur in a logical and efficient sequence in order to arrive at the most successful outcome.</p>
<p>Phases of deign:</p>
<ol>
<li>Recognition of need</li>
<li>Definition of problem - creation of the design requirements or design specifications (inputs, outputs, size, space, etc.)</li>
<li>Conceptual design - initial ideas</li>
<li>Concept development - Synthesis or feasibility analysis</li>
<li>Detailed design - Analysis and optimization</li>
<li>Evaluation - prototype manufacture and testing</li>
<li>Presentation - manufacture</li>
</ol>
<p>A prototype is an original type, form, or instance of something serving as a typical example, basis, or standard for other things of the same category.</p>
<p>In many fields, there is great uncertainty as to whether a new design will actually do what is desired. New designs often have unexpected problems. A prototype is often used as part of the product design process to allow engineers and designers the ability to explore design alternatives, test theories and confirm performance prior to starting production of a new product. Engineers use their experience to tailor the prototype according to the specific unknowns still present in the intended design. For example, some prototypes are used to confirm and verify consumer interest in a proposed design where as other prototypes will attempt to verify the performance or suitability of a specific design approach.</p>
<p>In general, an iterative series of prototypes will be designed, constructed and tested as the final design emerges and is prepared for production. With rare exceptions, multiple iterations of prototypes are used to progressively refine the design. A common strategy is to design, test, evaluate and then modify the design based on analysis of the prototype.</p>
<p>In many products it is common to assign the prototype iterations Greek letters. For example, a first iteration prototype may be called an ”Alpha” prototype. Often this iteration is not expected to perform as intended and some amount of failures or issues are anticipated. Subsequent prototyping</p>
<p>EME 2101: 8.2 Introduction
78</p>
<p>iterations (Beta, Gamma, etc.) will be expected to resolve issues and perform closer to the final production intent.</p>
<p>In many product development organizations, prototyping specialists are employed - individuals with specialized skills and training in general fabrication techniques that can help bridge between theoretical designs and the fabrication of prototypes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-2-introduction">8.2 Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-2-introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A free hand drawing or sketching is a drawing created without the use of drawing instruments. Free hand sketching allows at least a designer or drafter to convey ideas quickly and easily. It also helps in the conceptualization phase of the design process:</p>
<ul>
<li>How we picture objects in our minds.</li>
<li>How we visualize spatial relationships</li>
</ul>
<p>Sketching materials:</p>
<p>i. Soft pencil such as HB or B
ii. Paper
iii. Cross-section paper (with uniform squares) - helpful for sketching to scale.
iv. For isometric sketching, a specially ruled isometric paper may be used.
v. Good quality eraser</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-3-types-of-sketches">8.3 Types of sketches<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-3-types-of-sketches" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The form of any technical sketch should conform to one of the three standard types of drawings:</p>
<ol>
<li>Multi-view</li>
<li>Pictorial</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>axonometric (isometric)</li>
<li>oblique</li>
<li>perspective</li>
</ul>
<ol start="3">
<li>diagrammatic</li>
</ol>
<p>This document is available on
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com</a>)</p>
<p>EME 2101: 8.3 Types of sketches
79</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-3-1-scale-proportion">8.3.1 Scale/ Proportion<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-3-1-scale-proportion" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Sketches are usually not made to scale but should be made in their correct proportions as accurately as possible. Proportion is the relationship between the size of one part to another and to the object as a whole. In some cases, a cross-section paper may be used to assist in sketching to correct proportions. The size of a sketch is normally optional, depending upon the complexity of the object and the size of paper available. Small objects are often sketched oversize so as to show the necessary details clearly.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-3-2-sketching-tips">8.3.2 Sketching Tips<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-3-2-sketching-tips" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Hold pencil loosely so that it has a free and easy movement.</li>
<li>Rotate pencil to maintain a sharp point</li>
<li>Practice using different pressure to produce desired line types.</li>
<li>Draw in a direction comfortable to you.</li>
<li>Reorient paper to your convenience</li>
<li>Do NOT use a straight edge to draw lines!</li>
<li>Mark end points of the lines to be sketched and draw between them</li>
<li>Draw long lines as a series of short light ones and then darken them to obtain the finished lines.</li>
<li>Begin by drawing bounding boxes with construction lines</li>
<li>Make construction lines much lighter and thinner than finished lines</li>
<li>Identify the major features</li>
<li>Leave construction lines on the sketch</li>
<li>Use grid paper when available.</li>
<li>Include written notes and dimensions to aid in interpretation.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-3-3-multi-view-sketching">8.3.3 Multi view sketching<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-3-3-multi-view-sketching" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>A drawing for use in production should contain only those views needed for a clear and complete shape description of the object. These minimum required views are referred to as the <strong>necessary views</strong>.</li>
<li>In selecting views, choose those views that show best, the essential contours or shapes and give preference to those with least number of hidden details.</li>
<li>Very thin extrude parts may be shown using one view and a note added to indicate the thickness</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrick.ngobiro@student.moringaschool.com</a>)</p>
<p>EME 2101: 8.3 Types of sketches
80</p>
<p>of the part.</p>
<ul>
<li>Cylindrical objects can be shown using two views, a circular and a rectangular view.</li>
<li>Always try to maintain the natural orientation of the object.</li>
<li>Keep views aligned</li>
</ul>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="conventional-representation-of-features">Conventional Representation of Features<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#conventional-representation-of-features" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="9-1-introduction">9.1 Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#9-1-introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Drawing of most engineering components is tedious and consumes a lot of time. The primary object of the use of conventions is to save the draughtsman time and in some cases to save on space. A set of representations have been developed and standardized to show commonly used engineering components. The following conventions are selected from BS 8888 and ISO 2203.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>General:</th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Housing:</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>A component into which a</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>'male' mating part fits, sits or</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>is 'housed'.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Bush/bearing:</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>A removable sleeve or liner.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Known also as a simple or</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>plane bearing.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Boss:</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>A cylindrical projection on</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>surface of component.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Curved slot:</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Elongated hole, whose</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>centerline lies on an arc.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Used usually on components</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>requiring adjustment.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Rib:</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>A reinforcement, positioned</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>to stiffen surfaces.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Fillet:</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>A radius or rounded portion</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>suppressing a sharp internal</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>corner.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Key:</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>A small block or wedge</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>inserted between a shaft and</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>a mating part (a hub). Used</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>to prevent relative rotation of</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>the two parts.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Key way:</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>A parallel sided slot or</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>groove cut into a bore or a</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>shaft, to 'house' a mating</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>key.</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Figure 9.1:</p>

Bolts have a shank partially threaded, whereas screws are threaded along the entire length.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>For guidance on dimensioning, see next page.</td><td>HEXAGON</td></tr><tr><td>HEAD</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>BOLT</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>HEXAGON</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>HEAD</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>SCREW</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>STUD</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>The last three examples here are called set screws and are used to position or lock components.</td><td>Round head</td></tr><tr><td>Fillister head</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Instrument screw</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Countersunk head</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>

91</p>
<p>|  <strong>Splines:</strong>
Usually used for transmitting rotational torque and allowing an axial 'sliding' movement
Examples can be found on automotive drive shafts.</p>
